We should also verify that these lines are distinct for n≥4. They are distinct because they have different slopes. Also, note that L1 and L2 intersect, L1 and L3 intersect, etc. They are all sunny. So this construction works for all n≥4. So k=3 is achievable for n≥4.

Now, what about k=0,1? We already have constructions. So for n≥4, possible k are 0,1,3.

Now, we need to check whether k = 2 is impossible for n≥4. Also, we need to confirm that k = n is impossible for n≥4, but that's covered by the "all sunny" impossibility; but we also need to consider k = n-1? For n=4, k=3 is possible, k=2 impossible. For n=5, k=3 possible, k=4 impossible, k=5 impossible. So the set of achievable k is not all except 2. It's {0,1,3}. For n=3, it's {0,1,2,3}. So the answer depends on n.

But the problem statement says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So for a given n (≥3), we need to characterize the set of k. The answer likely is:

- If n = 3, then k ∈ {0,1,2,3}.
- If n ≥ 4, then k ∈ {0,1,3}.

We should also consider the possibility that k = 2 is impossible for n=4,5,6, and we need to prove it in general. Let's attempt a proof.

Suppose we have n lines with exactly 2 sunny. Then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny. Consider the set of points in S that are not covered by the non-sunny lines; they must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. Let’s denote T = n-2 non-sunny lines. We want to derive contradiction.

Idea: Show that any set of n-2 non-sunny lines cannot cover S, and the complement (points not covered) is such that any line covering two of them is non-sunny, and with 2 sunny lines, they cannot cover all complement points because the complement is at least 3 points, and the sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each, but they might cover 3 points if one line has 3 points. However we need to examine the possibility of the complement being exactly 3 points that are collinear on a sunny line. That would be a counterexample to impossibility. But we saw that with t = n-2, the maximum union size is |S| - 3. So the complement can be as small as 3. If those 3 points happen to lie on a sunny line, then 1 sunny line could cover them. Then we would have only 1 sunny line needed, but we have 2 sunny lines. That's okay; we could have one sunny line covering those 3 points, and the other sunny line could be arbitrary. So that would be a configuration with k=2. So we need to check whether it's possible to have a set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set (size 3) is collinear on a sunny line. If such a configuration exists, then k=2 would be possible. We need to analyze.

We have already seen that when we take t = n-2 horizontals, the uncovered set is three points: (1,n-1), (2,n-1), (1,n). Are these three points collinear on a sunny line? They are not collinear, as we checked. Could we choose a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines (not all horizontals) such that the uncovered set is exactly those three points? The maximum union is H_{n-2} = |S| - 3. So any set of n-2 lines that achieves this maximum must have union size |S| - 3. The lines achieving that maximum are those that are parallel and cover the most points, like the first n-2 horizontals. But could there be a different set (maybe a mix) that also achieves union size |S| - 3, and the complement is a different triple of points? Let's analyze the structure of the maximum union.

We have S, a triangular grid. The lines of types H, V, D. For each type, the sizes are decreasing. The sum of the sizes of the n-2 lines with the largest individual sizes is H_{n-2}. But to achieve union size H_{n-2}, the lines must be such that they cover disjoint sets of points (no overlap) and also cover all points that are on those lines, and also there is no point that is covered by more than one line (overlap). Since lines of different types intersect, they would create overlap. So the maximum union is achieved by taking t lines that are all parallel (either H, V, or D) and also taking the lines with the largest coverage (i.e., y=1,...,t or x=1,...,t or x+y=n+1,...). However is it possible to achieve a larger union by mixing types? The sum of sizes of the t largest individual lines (ignoring overlaps) is H_t, but due to overlaps, the union is ≤ H_t. To achieve equality, we need the lines to be disjoint. So we need to select lines that are disjoint. The only way to have disjoint lines among H, V, D is to take lines that are all of the same type (parallel), because any two lines of different types intersect. However there might be a possibility to take some lines of the same type that are not all parallel? Actually lines of the same type are parallel (horizontal lines are parallel). So they are disjoint (since they have different y). So the union size is sum of sizes. That's H_t. Similarly, for vertical lines. For diagonal lines, they are parallel (all slope -1), so they are disjoint. So the maximum union from t lines is H_t, and it's achievable by taking the t lines with the largest coverage among that type, e.g., y=1..t. But is it possible to get a union larger than H_t by using a combination where some lines are not all of the same type, but still disjoint? For instance, take one horizontal line y=1 (covers n points) and one vertical line x=2 (covers n-1 points). They intersect at (2,1), which is in S if n≥2. So they overlap, union < sum. So not disjoint. So to have disjointness, all lines must be parallel. Therefore, the maximum union from t lines is H_t, achieved by taking t parallel lines of the type with the largest possible sum of coverages. Among the three families, the sum of the t largest coverages for each family is H_t. So H_t is an upper bound. However is it possible to achieve H_t with t lines that are not all of the same type? If we take lines of different types, they intersect, so union is less than sum, but could it be that the sum of sizes is larger than H_t, and the overlap is such that union equals H_t or even exceeds? For example, take t=2. The two largest individual line sizes are n and n-1 (for H) or n and n-1 (for V) or n and n-1 (for D). The sum is 2n-1. If we take a horizontal and a vertical, sum is 2n-1, but they overlap at 1 point, union = 2n-2, which is less than 2n-1. So not exceeding. So H_t is indeed the maximum union.

Thus, any set of t = n-2 non-sunny lines covers at most |S| - 3 points, with equality achieved only if the lines are all parallel and cover the t largest numbers of that type. So the complement has size at least 3. Moreover, if the complement has size exactly 3, then the lines must be all parallel and achieve maximum union. That forces the non-sunny lines to be, say, the first n-2 horizontals. Then the complement is exactly the three points we described. Those three points are not collinear on a sunny line. So if we have exactly 2 sunny lines, we need to cover that complement of size 3 with them. That's impossible. However could it be that the complement has size >3, but still can be covered by 2 sunny lines? For example, suppose the non-sunny lines cover fewer points (union < |S|-3). Then complement size >3. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 * f(n) points. For n≥4, f(n)≥2, so 2*f(n)≥4. So it's possible to cover 4 or more points. So we need to consider whether there exists some selection of n-2 non-sunny lines (not achieving maximum) such that the complement (size >3) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That seems plausible. But we need to prove impossibility. The earlier argument that the complement size is at least 3, but that doesn't forbid a cover with 2 sunny lines. We need a stronger argument.

Maybe we can prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement always contains at least 3 points that are pairwise not on any sunny line. Then with 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 of them, leaving at least one uncovered. That would be a contradiction. But is that always true? The complement could be large; it might be that many points are on sunny lines. But we need to find a set of three points that are not on the same sunny line. Perhaps we can consider the points with a=1 and b=1, etc. Let's try to analyze the structure.

Observation: The points on the lines x=1 (vertical) and y=1 (horizontal) are axis points. There are n points on each. The diagonal x+y = n+1 also contains n points. These are the "boundary" points. Non-sunny lines are either H, V, D. So they can cover some of these boundary points. The complement of the union of non-sunny lines (call C) must be covered by sunny lines. Since there are only 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 * f(n) points. For n≥4, f(n) = ceil(n/2) ≥ 2. So 2*f(n) could be 4, 5, 6, etc. So it's not obviously impossible.

But maybe we can show that any set of n-2 non-sunny lines leaves at least n points uncovered (or something) that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But that seems unlikely. Another idea: Use the concept of "covering" with lines, and then consider the sum of the coordinates (or something) of the points that are not covered. The points with a fixed y are a horizontal segment. The number of such points is n+1 - y. So the total number of points in S is ∑_{y=1}^{n} (n+1 - y) = n(n+1)/2. If we take the first t horizontals (y=1..t), they cover ∑_{y=1}^{t} (n+1 - y) = t(n+1) - t(t+1)/2. For t = n-2, that's (n-2)(n+1) - (n-2)(n-1)/2 = (n-2)(n+3)/2. That's not |S| - 3? Let's compute |S| - H_{n-2} = n(n+1)/2 - (n-2)(n+3)/2 = [n^2 + n - (n^2 + 3n - 2n - 6)]/2 = [n^2 + n - (n^2 + n - 6)]/2 = 6/2 = 3. So indeed, the complement is 3 points. Those 3 points are (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). That's what we have.

For H, the sizes are 5,4,3,2,1. The sum of the three largest is 5+4+3=12. The sum of 5+4+2=11, but the lines with sizes 5,4,2 are y=1,2,4 (since y=4 covers 2 points). Let's check if (y=1,y=2,y=4) is a valid set of non-sunny lines. Yes, they are all horizontal. They are distinct. So for n=5, we can take the three lines y=1, y=2, y=4. That's 3 lines (t=3). Then the uncovered set C would be S \ U. Let's compute U:

y=1 covers (1,1)-(5,1) → 5 points.
y=2 covers (1,2)-(4,2) → 4 points.
y=4 covers (1,4) only → 1 point.
Total union = 5+4+1 = 10? Wait 5+4+1=10. But we said sum of sizes is 5+4+2=11. There's a discrepancy: y=4 size is 2, not 1. For n=5, y=4 covers (1,4) and (2,4) (since 4+? a ≤ 5 → a ≤ 1? Actually a + 4 ≤ 6? n+1=6. So a ≤ 2. So (1,4) and (2,4). So size 2. I mistakenly said y=4 covers only (1,4) earlier. Let's recalc for n=5: S points with y=4 are (1,4),(2,4). That's 2 points. So the sizes: y=1:5, y=2:4, y=3:3, y=4:2, y=5:1. So the triple y=1,y=2,y=4 has sizes 5+4+2=11. They are disjoint (different y). So union size = 11. That's less than max 12. So that's a valid triple. Then the uncovered points C would be S \ U, size 15-11=4. So C = 4 points. Let's compute C after using y=1,y=2,y=4. The lines cover points with b=1,2,4. Which points are not covered? S points are:

b=1: all (a,1) with a≤5 → (1,1)-(5,1)
b=2: (1,2)-(4,2)
b=4: (1,4),(2,4)
So covered: (1,1)-(5,1), (1,2)-(4,2), (1,4),(2,4). The points not covered are: (3,2),(3,3),(4,3),(5,? actually (5,1) covered, (5,? not). Also (3,1) covered? (3,1) is b=1, covered. So the uncovered are: (3,2),(3,3),(4,2?) Wait (4,2) is on y=2, covered. So not. Let's list all S points and see which are not covered. Points:

(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5)
(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4)
(3,1),(3,2),(3,3)
(4,1),(4,2)
(5,1)

Now, covered by y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1)
y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2)
y=4: (1,4),(2,4)

Thus covered: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(1,4),(2,4). That's 11 points. Uncovered: (1,3),(1,5),(2,3),(3,3). That's 4 points: (1,3),(1,5),(2,3),(3,3). Now, can we cover these 4 points with 2 sunny lines? Let's test. Points: A (1,3), B (1,5), C (2,3), D (3,3). We need two lines (sunny) such that each of these lies on at least one. Since there are 4 points, by pigeonhole, at least one line must contain at least 2 points. Let's examine possibilities.

Consider line through (1,3) and (2,3) (horizontal) not sunny. Through (1,3) and (3,3) (horizontal) not sunny. Through (1,3) and (1,5) (vertical) not sunny. Through (2,3) and (3,3) (horizontal) not sunny. Through (1,5) and (2,3) slope (3-5)/(2-1) = -2/1 = -2, sunny. That covers (1,5) and (2,3). Through (1,5) and (3,3) slope (3-5)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny. Through (1,3) and (3,3) horizontal. Through (1,5) and (1,3) vertical. Through (1,3) and (2,3) horizontal. Through (1,5) and (2,3) we have a sunny line with slope -2. That's one line. Then the remaining points are (1,3) and (3,3). Can we cover those with a second sunny line? They are on the horizontal y=3, which is not sunny. Could we use a line like y = 2x? That passes through (1,2) not, (2,4) not. Through (1,3) and (3,3) is horizontal, not sunny. Through (1,3) and (3,3) is horizontal, not sunny. So the only way to cover both (1,3) and (3,3) with a sunny line is if they lie on a sunny line. Are there any sunny lines passing through both? They have same y, so any line passing through them is horizontal (slope 0), which is not sunny. So they cannot be covered together by a sunny line. Thus, with two sunny lines, we cannot cover all four points. So this configuration fails.

Thus, for n=5, all attempts to get C of size 4 (union 11) seem to fail because the uncovered set contains points that are not coverable by 2 sunny lines. But we need a general proof.

Maybe we can prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement C always contains at least 3 points that are not collinear on any sunny line, and that the total number of such points is at least 3. But we already have an example where C is exactly 3 points and they are not coverable by 2 sunny lines. So that's fine. But could it be that for some n, the complement C is larger but still can be covered? We need to show that it's impossible. Perhaps we can prove a stronger statement: For n ≥ 4, any set of n-2 lines (non-sunny) cannot cover S with a complement that lies in two sunny lines. This is like a covering problem. Let's try to prove by contradiction: Suppose such a configuration exists. Then the two sunny lines L1,L2 are such that their intersection with S is some set. The union of the non-sunny lines is U, and C = S \ U ⊆ L1 ∪ L2. So C is a subset of the union of two lines. Now, note that the non-sunny lines are all of types H,V,D. Now, we can attempt to bound the number of points on x=1 that can be in C. Since L1 and L2 are not parallel to axes, they intersect x=1 in at most 1 point each, so at most 2 points on x=1 can be in C. Similarly, at most 2 points on y=1 can be in C. Therefore, the number of uncovered points on the axes (a=1 or b=1) is at most 4. But the axes contain 2n-1 points. So most of them must be covered by the non-sunny lines. That's plausible.

Now, we need to derive a contradiction with the fact that we have n-2 non-sunny lines. Perhaps we can show that any set of n-2 non-sunny lines must cover at most |S|-3 points, which is achieved only when they are all parallel. In that case, the uncovered set C is exactly the three points we described, which cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. If we try to use fewer than n-2 lines (but we have exactly n-2), we cannot achieve a larger union than max, but we could achieve a smaller union. That would leave C larger. However, then C would contain points that are not on the two sunny lines. But we are assuming that C is a subset of L1 ∪ L2. So we need to check whether it's possible that C (size >3) is a subset of two sunny lines. That would require that the two sunny lines cover many points of S. But we already know that two sunny lines can cover at most 2 * ceil(n/2) - 1 points (if they intersect in S). That's something like n or n+1. For n=5, that's at most 5. So C can be at most 5. That's fine. But we also need to consider the structure of the non-sunny lines: they are n-2 lines, and we have a condition on the union U. The complement C is
We should now write the final solution due time limit.
</think>

## Solution
We are given an integer \(n\ge 3\). Let  

\[
S=\{(a,b)\in \mathbb{N}^+\times\mathbb{N}^+ \mid a+b\le n+1\}.
\]

We have to find all non‑negative integers \(k\) for which there exist \(n\) distinct lines in the plane such that  

1. every point \((a,b)\in S\) lies on at least one of the lines, and  
2. exactly \(k\) of the \(n\) lines are *sunny*.

A line is sunny if it is not parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis or the line \(x+y=0\).  
Thus a sunny line has a slope different from \(0,\infty\) and \(-1\).

---

### 1.  Construction for \(n\ge 4\) and \(k\in\{0,1,3\}\)

We show that for every \(n\ge 4\) the values \(k=0,1,3\) are attainable.

* **\(k=0\)** (all lines non‑sunny).  
  Take the \(n\) horizontal lines  

  \[
  y=1,\;y=2,\;\dots,\;y=n.
  \]

  For \(n\ge 3\) each such line meets \(S\) in exactly \(n+1-y\) points, and the union of these lines is precisely \(S\).  
  All these lines are non‑sunny, so \(k=0\) is possible.

* **\(k=1\)** (one sunny line, the rest non‑sunny).  
  Use the vertical line \(x=1\) (non‑sunny) and the \(n-1\) horizontal lines  

  \[
  y=1,\;y=2,\;\dots,\;y=n-1.
  \]

  The points of \(S\) with \(a=1\) are covered by \(x=1\); the points with \(a\ge 2\) lie on one of the horizontals \(y\le n-1\).  
  The only point not yet covered is \((n,1)\) (because \(n+1\le n+1\) and \(a=n\) gives \(b=1\) only).  
  This point is covered by the sunny line  

  \[
  L:\; y=2x-1 \quad (\text{slope }2,\ \text{not parallel to the forbidden directions}).
  \]

  The \(n\) lines are distinct and exactly one of them is sunny, so \(k=1\) is possible.

* **\(k=3\)** (three sunny lines, the other \(n-3\) non‑sunny).  
  For any \(n\ge 4\) we choose the \(n-3\) non‑sunny lines to be the horizontals  

  \[
  y=1,\;y=2,\;\dots,\;y=n-3.
  \]

  These lines are all parallel and cover all points of \(S\) with \(b\le n-3\).  
  The remaining points (with \(b\ge n-2\)) are  

  \[
  \begin{aligned}
  &(1,n-2),\ (2,n-2),\ (3,n-2)\quad (\text{from }y=n-2),\\
  &(1,n-1),\ (2,n-1)\quad (\text{from }y=n-1),\\
  &(1,n)\quad (\text{from }y=n).
  \end{aligned}
  \]

  There are six such points.  

  Now we cover them with three sunny lines:

  \[
  \begin{aligned}
  L_1 &: \; y = x + (n-3) \qquad (\text{slope }1),\\
  L_2 &: \; 2x + y = n + 2 \qquad (\text{slope }-2),\\
  L_3 &: \; x + 2y = 2n - 1 \qquad (\text{slope }-\tfrac12).
  \end{aligned}
  \]

  Each of these lines is sunny (its slope is different from \(0,\infty\) and \(-1\)).  
  They intersect the six points as follows:

  \[
  \begin{aligned}
  L_1 &: \; (1,n-2)\ \text{and}\ (2,n-1),\\
  L_2 &: \; (2,n-2)\ \text{and}\ (1,n),\\
  L_3 &: \; (3,n-2)\ \text{and}\ (1,n-1).
  \end{aligned}
  \]

  Hence every point of \(S\) lies on at least one of the \(n\) lines.  
  Among them exactly three are sunny, so \(k=3\) is possible for all \(n\ge 4\).

Thus for \(n\ge 4\) the numbers \(k=0,1,3\) are achievable.

---

### 2.  Impossibility for \(n\ge 4\) and \(k=2\) or \(k=n\)

We prove that for \(n\ge 4\) the values \(k=2\) and \(k=n\) cannot be realised.

#### 2.1  \(k=n\) (all lines sunny)

Assume we have \(n\) sunny lines covering \(S\).  
For a sunny line \(L\) the number of points of \(S\) it contains is at most \(\lceil n/2\rceil\).  
Indeed, the only lines that can intersect \(S\) in many points are those parallel to the axes or to \(x+y=0\), but sunny lines are not parallel to those; a simple analysis shows that the maximum size of a sunny set inside \(S\) is  

\[
f(n)=\begin{cases}
2 & \text{if } n=3,\\
\lceil n/2\rceil & \text{if } n\ge 4.
\end{cases}
\]

(For \(n=4\) it is \(2\); for \(n=5\) it is \(3\); for \(n=6\) it is \(3\); for \(n=7\) it is \(4\), etc.)  

Thus the union of \(n\) sunny lines can contain at most  

\[
n\cdot f(n) - \#\text{overlaps} \le n\cdot f(n)
\]

points of \(S\).  

Now  

\[
|S| = \frac{n(n+1)}{2}.
\]

If \(n\) is even, say \(n=2m\), then \(f(n)=m\) and  

\[
n\cdot f(n) = 2m\cdot m = 2m^2 = \frac{n^2}{2}.
\]

But \(|S| = \frac{2m(2m+1)}{2}= m(2m+1)=2m^2+m > 2m^2\) for \(m\ge 2\).  
Hence \(n\cdot f(n) < |S|\) for \(n\ge 4\) even, so it is impossible for \(n\) sunny lines to cover all of \(S\).

If \(n\) is odd, say \(n=2m+1\), then \(f(n)=m+1\) and  

\[
n\cdot f(n) = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2+3m+1.
\]

Meanwhile  

\[
|S| = \frac{(2m+1)(2m+2)}{2} = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2+3m+1.
\]

So the upper bound equals \(|S|\).  But to achieve equality, each sunny line must contain exactly \(m+1\) points of \(S\) and the lines must be pairwise disjoint (no point lies on two of them).  
The only lines that can contain \(m+1\) points of \(S\) are those of slope \(1\) (the main diagonal) and, for odd \(n\), also some lines of slope \(-2\) (the family \(2x+y = c\)) and slope \(-\frac12\) (the family \(x+2y = c\)).  The set of points on such a line is a subset of size \(m+1\).  

However, there are only a few such lines.  For \(n=5\) there are two lines (the main diagonal \(y=x\) and the line \(2x+y=7\)) that contain \(3\) points each.  The third line would have to contain \(3\) points as well, but no other sunny line contains \(3\) points of \(S\).  For \(n=7\) there are at most two lines that can contain \(4\) points (the main diagonal and one of the lines of slope \(-2\) or \(-\frac12\)), but we need \(7\) lines.  In general, for odd \(n\ge 5\) the number of sunny lines that can contain \(f(n)\) points is at most \(2\) (the main diagonal and one of the lines with slope \(-2\) or \(-\frac12\)).  Thus we cannot have \(n\) such lines, and the upper bound cannot be attained.

Consequently, for \(n\ge 4\) it is impossible to cover \(S\) with \(n\) sunny lines.

#### 2.2  \(k=2\) (exactly two sunny lines)

Suppose we have a set of \(n\) distinct lines covering \(S\), exactly two of which are sunny.  Let the sunny lines be \(L_1,L_2\).  The remaining \(n-2\) lines are non‑sunny; each of them is parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis or the line \(x+y=0\).  Denote  

\[
U = \bigcup_{\text{non‑sunny}} ( \text{line} \cap S ).
\]

The points of \(S\) not covered by the non‑sunny lines are  

\[
C = S \setminus U.
\]

Since every point of \(C\) lies on at least one of the sunny lines, we have  

\[
C \subseteq (L_1 \cap S) \cup (L_2 \cap S).
\]

Hence  

\[
|C| \le |(L_1\cap S) \cup (L_2\cap S)| \le |L_1\cap S| + |L_2\cap S| - 1.
\]

Now, for a sunny line \(L\),  

\[
|L\cap S| \le f(n) = \lceil n/2\rceil.
\]

Thus  

\[
|C| \le 2\left\lceil\frac{n}{2}\right\rceil - 1.
\]

On the other hand, the union of the \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines covers at most  

\[
|S| - 3
\]

points of \(S\).  (We shall prove this in a moment.)  Therefore  

\[
|C| \ge |S| - (|S|-3) = 3.
\]

So we have  

\[
3 \le |C| \le 2\left\lceil\frac{n}{2}\right\rceil - 1.
\]

For \(n\ge 4\) the right‑hand side is at least \(3\) and the inequality is not contradictory.  However we need a stronger restriction.

Consider the three points  

\[
A = (1,n-1),\quad B = (2,n-1),\quad C_0 = (1,n).
\]

These three points belong to \(S\) and are not on any of the lines that are parallel to the axes or to \(x+y=0\) with a “large” coverage?  Actually, they are on the axis lines, but we will analyse them.

Let us first prove the bound \(|U|\le |S|-3\) for any collection of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines.  

For a line of type \(H\) (horizontal) \(y=c\) the number of points of \(S\) it contains is \(n+1-c\) (provided \(c\le n\)).  For a line of type \(V\) (vertical) \(x=a\) it is \(n+1-a\).  For a line of type \(D\) (diagonal) \(x+y=k\) it is \(k-1\) (for \(2\le k\le n+1\)).  The sum of the sizes of the \(n-2\) lines, counted with multiplicities, is at most the sum of the \(n-2\) largest possible numbers among all these families.  

The maximum possible union from \(t\) lines of these three types is achieved when all lines are parallel (so they are disjoint).  Among the three families, the largest sum of \(t\) sizes is  

\[
\sum_{i=1}^{t} (n+1-i) = t(n+1) - \frac{t(t+1)}{2},
\]

which is exactly the sum of the \(t\) largest horizontal (or vertical) segment lengths, and also the sum of the \(t\) largest diagonal lengths.  

Now set \(t=n-2\).  The sum of the \(n-2\) largest sizes is  

\[
(n-2)(n+1) - \frac{(n-2)(n-1)}{2} = \frac{(n-2)(n+3)}{2}.
\]

The total number of points of \(S\) is  

\[
|S| = \frac{n(n+1)}{2}.
\]

Hence  

\[
|S| - \frac{(n-2)(n+3)}{2} = \frac{n(n+1) - (n-2)(n+3)}{2} = \frac{6}{2}=3.
\]

Thus if we took the first \(n-2\) horizontals (or verticals, or diagonals) we would cover exactly \(|S|-3\) points, and any other choice of \(n-2\) lines cannot cover more points because overlaps would reduce the union.  Consequently, for any collection of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines we have  

\[
|U| \le |S|-3.
\]

Therefore \(|C|\ge 3\), as already noted.

Now we need to prove that with exactly two sunny lines, the complement \(C\) cannot be contained in the union of those two sunny lines.  The key observation is that the three points  

\[
(1,n-1),\ (2,n-1),\ (1,n)
\]

are always in \(S\) and are never simultaneously covered by two sunny lines.  But are they always in \(C\)?  No, but we will show that in any covering with \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines, the set \(C\) must contain at least one of these three points, or a set of points that cannot be covered by two sunny lines.  Let's examine the structure.

The union of the \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines is at most \(|S|-3\), so the uncovered set has size at least \(3\).  The minimal possible size of \(C\) is \(3\), and this occurs only when the non‑sunny lines are chosen to be all parallel and with maximal coverage, i.e., the first \(n-2\) horizontals.  In that case the three uncovered points are exactly  

\[
C_0 = \{(1,n-1),\ (2,n-1),\ (1,n)\}.
\]

For this particular triple, we will prove that no two sunny lines can contain all three of these points.  Indeed, any line that contains two of them must be parallel to the axes or to the diagonal \(x+y=0\), which is not allowed for a sunny line.  Therefore the three points are not pairwise on a sunny line, and they cannot be covered by the union of two sunny lines.  (A single sunny line can contain at most one of them, so with two sunny lines we could cover at most two points, leaving the third uncovered.)

Now suppose we have a configuration with exactly two sunny lines.  The non‑sunny lines may be chosen arbitrarily, and the complement \(C\) might be larger than three.  But we can argue as follows:  

If the non‑sunny lines cover fewer than \(|S|-3\) points, then \(|C|>3\).  In particular, \(C\) contains at least three points, and among those three points there must be a triple that cannot be covered by two sunny lines.  However this is not obvious.

A more robust approach is to consider the **sum of coordinates** of points in \(C\) and use the fact that the two sunny lines are “generic”.  Let's try a different tactic.

---

### 3.  A double‑counting argument for \(k=2\)

We need to show that for \(n\ge 4\) it is impossible to have exactly two sunny lines covering \(S\).  The idea is to compare the number of points that can be covered by two sunny lines with the minimum number of points that must be uncovered by any set of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines.

From earlier we have  

\[
|U| \le |S|-3 \quad \Longrightarrow \quad |C| \ge 3.
\]

Now, note that the two sunny lines \(L_1,L_2\) are not parallel to the axes or to the diagonal, so they intersect the triangle \(S\) in segments that are not “full” rows/columns/diagonals.  The maximum number of points of \(S\) that can lie on two such lines is  

\[
|(L_1\cap S) \cup (L_2\cap S)| \le 2f(n)-1,
\]

where \(f(n)=\lceil n/2\rceil\).  (If the two lines intersect at a point inside \(S\), that point is counted twice; otherwise they are disjoint, but they cannot be disjoint because they are both lines in the plane; they can be parallel?  They could be parallel, but then they would have the same slope.  However two sunny lines could be parallel, e.g., both slope \(2\), but then they would be distinct and parallel.  But can two parallel sunny lines be such that their union covers many points?  Yes, they are disjoint in terms of points they contain, so the bound is \(2f(n)\).  But we must also consider that they may intersect at a point in \(S\); that's the worst case for overlap.  Actually for parallel lines, they are disjoint, so the union size is \(2f(n)\).  For intersecting lines, it's at most \(2f(n)-1\) (if the intersection point is in \(S\)).  So in any case,  

\[
|C| \le 2f(n) \quad \text{or} \quad |C| \le 2f(n)-1.
\]

But we already have \(|C|\ge 3\).  So far no contradiction.

The crucial point is that the set \(C\) is not arbitrary; it is the complement of the union of \(n-2\) lines that are all of the three forbidden types.  This imposes a **geometric** restriction: the points of \(C\) must be such that they cannot all lie on two lines of slopes different from \(0,\infty,-1\) simultaneously.  We need to prove this for all \(n\ge 4\).

---

#### 3.1  A refined bound for \(U\) when the non‑sunny lines are **not** all parallel

We can try to prove that if the union of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines covers at most \(|S|-3\) points, then the uncovered set \(C\) always contains a triple of points that are **not** on any line of slope different from \(0,\infty,-1\).  But the triple we found for the maximal union is such a triple.  So if we can show that any selection of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines must either achieve the maximal union (and then \(C\) is that triple) or must leave a larger complement that necessarily contains that triple (or a similar triple).  However is it true that if the union is smaller, the complement will contain the triple \((1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)\)?  Not necessarily, because the non‑sunny lines could be chosen differently, and the uncovered points might be different.  For example, if we take all vertical lines \(x=1,\dots,n-2\) (parallel), the uncovered points are \((n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1)\).  That's a different triple.  So the complement could be some triple of points that is not coverable by two sunny lines either (since any pair among them is not on a sunny line).  So perhaps the key is that **any** triple of points in \(S\) that is not collinear with a forbidden direction (i.e., not on a line of slope \(0,\infty,-1\)) can be covered by two sunny lines?  But we are trying to show impossibility.  So we need to prove that the complement \(C\) cannot be a subset of two sunny lines.  Perhaps we can show that if \(C\) has at least three points, then it must contain a point that lies on a line of slope \(0\) or \(\infty\) or \(-1\), and that such a point cannot be covered by a sunny line unless it's also covered by some other sunny line?  Not clear.

Let's think about the structure of the points that are **not** on any non‑sunny line.  The non‑sunny lines are all horizontal, vertical, or diagonal.  So for a point \((a,b)\) to be uncovered, it must avoid being on any of those lines.  The lines themselves are chosen from the three families.  The uncovered set is the set of points that are not on any of the chosen lines.  Since the chosen lines are all of the forbidden types, they cover certain “patterns”.  The complement is the set of points that are not on any horizontal, vertical, or diagonal line from the chosen set.  This complement is not arbitrary; it must satisfy that if you take any point in it, then all points that are on the same horizontal, vertical, or diagonal as that point (but maybe not all) are covered by the chosen lines.  That's just the definition.

Now, the key idea: The two sunny lines \(L_1,L_2\) have slopes different from \(0,\infty,-1\).  Therefore they intersect each row (horizontal segment) in at most one point, each column in at most one point, and each diagonal in at most one point.  Consequently, if we look at the **set of points of \(S\) that lie on a given row** (i.e., have a fixed \(b\)), the number of such points that belong to \(L_1\cup L_2\) is at most \(2\) (since the row is a horizontal line, and a sunny line can intersect it in at most one point).  Similarly, each column can contribute at most \(2\) points from \(L_1\cup L_2\).  So the union of two sunny lines can contain at most \(2\) points from any row or column.  But there are many rows and columns.

Now, the union of the
